</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_food_imports_and_exports">International food imports and exports</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Population_density" title="Population density">Population density</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> reported that the European Union was the top food importer in 2005, followed at a distance by the USA and Japan. <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">Britain</a>'s need for food was especially well illustrated in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. Despite the implementation of <a href="/wiki/Rationing_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Rationing in the United Kingdom">food rationing</a>, Britain remained dependent on food imports and the result was a long term engagement in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Atlantic" title="Battle of the Atlantic">Battle of the Atlantic</a>.</p>
<p>Food is traded and marketed on a global basis. The variety and availability of food is no longer restricted by the diversity of locally grown food or the limitations of the local growing season.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Between 1961 and 1999, there was a 400% increase in worldwide food exports.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Some countries are now economically dependent on food exports, which in some cases account for over 80% of all exports.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1994, over 100 countries became signatories to the <a href="/wiki/Uruguay_Round" title="Uruguay Round">Uruguay Round</a> of the <a href="/wiki/General_Agreement_on_Tariffs_and_Trade" title="General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade">General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade</a> in a dramatic increase in <a href="/wiki/Trade_liberalization" class="mw-redirect" title="Trade liberalization">trade liberalization</a>. This included an agreement to reduce subsidies paid to farmers, underpinned by the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">WTO</a> enforcement of <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_policy" title="Agricultural policy">agricultural subsidy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tax,_tariff_and_trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Tax, tariff and trade">tariffs</a>, import <a href="/wiki/Quota_Share" class="mw-redirect" title="Quota Share">quotas</a>, and settlement of trade disputes that cannot be bilaterally resolved.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Where trade barriers are raised on the disputed grounds of public health and safety, the WTO refer the dispute to the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Alimentarius" title="Codex Alimentarius">Codex Alimentarius</a> Commission, which was founded in 1962 by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> and the World Health Organization. Trade liberalization has greatly affected world food trade.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marketing_and_retailing">Marketing and retailing</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fredmeyer_edit_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Fredmeyer_edit_1.jpg/260px-Fredmeyer_edit_1.jpg" width="260" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Fredmeyer_edit_1.jpg/390px-Fredmeyer_edit_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Fredmeyer_edit_1.jpg/520px-Fredmeyer_edit_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2790" data-file-height="1835" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fredmeyer_edit_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Packaged food aisles of supermarket in <a href="/wiki/Portland,_Oregon" title="Portland, Oregon">Portland, Oregon</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States of America</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Food_marketing" title="Food marketing">Food marketing</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Food_marketing" title="Food marketing">Food marketing</a> brings together the producer and the consumer. It is the chain of activities that brings food from "farm gate to plate".<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The marketing of even a single food product can be a complicated process involving many producers and companies. For example, fifty-six companies are involved in making one <a href="/wiki/Canning" title="Canning">can</a> of chicken noodle soup. These businesses include not only chicken and vegetable processors but also the companies that transport the ingredients and those who print labels and manufacture cans.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The food marketing system is the largest direct and indirect non-government employer in the United States.</p>
<p>In the pre-modern era, the sale of surplus food took place once a week when farmers took their wares on market day into the local village marketplace. Here food was sold to <a href="/wiki/Grocer" class="mw-redirect" title="Grocer">grocers</a> for sale in their local shops for purchase by local consumers.<sup id="cite_ref-silhnc_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-silhnc-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jango_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jango-44">[44]</a></sup> With the onset of industrialization and the development of the food processing industry, a wider range of food could be sold and distributed in distant locations. Typically early grocery shops would be counter-based shops, in which purchasers told the shop-keeper what they wanted, so that the shop-keeper could get it for them.<sup id="cite_ref-silhnc_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-silhnc-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Supermarket" title="Supermarket">supermarkets</a> were born. Supermarkets brought with them a <a href="/wiki/Self_service" class="mw-redirect" title="Self service">self service</a> approach to shopping using <a href="/wiki/Shopping_cart" title="Shopping cart">shopping carts</a>, and were able to offer quality food at lower cost through <a href="/wiki/Economies_of_scale" title="Economies of scale">economies of scale</a> and reduced staffing costs. In the latter part of the 20th century, this has been further revolutionized by the development of vast warehouse-sized, out-of-town supermarkets, selling a wide range of food from around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike food processors, food retailing is a two-tier market in which a small number of very large <a href="/wiki/Corporation" title="Corporation">companies</a> control a large proportion of supermarkets. The supermarket giants wield great purchasing power over farmers and processors, and strong influence over consumers. Nevertheless, less than 10% of consumer spending on food goes to farmers, with larger percentages going to <a href="/wiki/Advertising" title="Advertising">advertising</a>, transportation, and intermediate corporations.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prices">Prices</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GrainProducts.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/GrainProducts.jpg/220px-GrainProducts.jpg" width="220" height="329" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/GrainProducts.jpg/330px-GrainProducts.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/GrainProducts.jpg/440px-GrainProducts.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1867" data-file-height="2790" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GrainProducts.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Some essential food products including <a href="/wiki/Bread" title="Bread">bread</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pasta" title="Pasta">pasta</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%932008_world_food_price_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2007–2008 world food price crisis">2007–2008 world food price crisis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Food_vs._fuel" title="Food vs. fuel">Food vs. fuel</a></div>
<p>It was reported on March 24, 2008, that consumers worldwide faced rising food prices.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn24march2008_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn24march2008-56">[56]</a></sup> Reasons for this development include changes in the weather and dramatic changes in the <a href="/wiki/Global_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Global economy">global economy</a>, including higher <a href="/wiki/Oil_price" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil price">oil prices</a>, lower food reserves, and growing consumer demand in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn24march2008_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn24march2008-56">[56]</a></sup> In the long term, prices are expected to stabilize.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn24march2008_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn24march2008-56">[56]</a></sup> Farmers will grow more grain for both <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> and food and eventually bring prices down.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn24march2008_56-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn24march2008-56">[56]</a></sup> Already this is happening with <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> with more <a href="/wiki/Crops" class="mw-redirect" title="Crops">crops</a> to be planted in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> in 2009. However, the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> projects that consumers still have to deal with more expensive food until at least 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn24march2008_56-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn24march2008-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Annual_real_food_price_indices.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Annual_real_food_price_indices.svg/220px-Annual_real_food_price_indices.svg.png" width="220" height="107" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Annual_real_food_price_indices.svg/330px-Annual_real_food_price_indices.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Annual_real_food_price_indices.svg/440px-Annual_real_food_price_indices.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="370" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Annual_real_food_price_indices.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Food, meat, dairy, cereals, vegetable oil, and sugar price indices, deflated using the World Bank Manufactures Unit Value Index (MUV)<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It is rare for the spikes to hit all major foods in most countries at once. Food prices rose 4% in the United States in 2007, the highest increase since 1990, and are expected to climb as much again in 2008. As of December 2007, 37 countries faced food crises, and 20 had imposed some sort of food-price controls. In China, the price of <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">pork</a> jumped 58% in 2007. In the 1980s and 1990s, farm subsidies and support programs allowed major grain exporting countries to hold large surpluses, which could be tapped during food shortages to keep prices down. However, new trade policies have made agricultural production much more responsive to market demands, putting global food reserves at their lowest since 1983.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn24march2008_56-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn24march2008-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Food prices are rising, wealthier Asian consumers are westernizing their diets, and farmers and nations of the third world are struggling to keep up the pace. The past five years have seen rapid growth in the contribution of Asian nations to the global fluid and powdered milk manufacturing industry, which in 2008 accounted for more than 30% of production, while China alone accounts for more than 10% of both production and consumption in the global fruit and vegetable processing and preserving industry. The trend is similarly evident in industries such as soft drink and bottled water manufacturing, as well as global cocoa, chocolate, and sugar confectionery manufacturing, forecast to grow by 5.7% and 10.0% respectively during 2008 in response to soaring demand in Chinese and Southeast Asian markets.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rising food prices over recent years have been linked with social unrest around the world, including rioting in <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Ind_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ind-61">[61]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 2013 <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Development_Institute" title="Overseas Development Institute">Overseas Development Institute</a> researchers showed that <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> has more than doubled in price since 2000, rising by 120% in real terms. This was as a result of shifts in trade policy and restocking by major producers. More fundamental drivers of increased prices are the higher costs of fertiliser, diesel and labour. Parts of Asia see rural wages rise with potential large benefits for the 1.3 billion (2008 estimate) of Asia's poor in reducing the poverty they face. However, this negatively impacts more vulnerable groups who don't share in the economic boom, especially in Asian and African coastal cities. The researchers said the threat means social-protection policies are needed to guard against price shocks. The research proposed that in the longer run, the rises present opportunities to export for Western African farmers with high potential for rice production to replace imports with domestic production.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_investment">As investment</span></h3>
<p>Institutions such as <a href="/wiki/Hedge_fund" title="Hedge fund">hedge funds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pension_fund" title="Pension fund">pension funds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Investment_bank" class="mw-redirect" title="Investment bank">investment banks</a> like <a href="/wiki/Barclays_Capital" class="mw-redirect" title="Barclays Capital">Barclays Capital</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs" title="Goldman Sachs">Goldman Sachs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley" title="Morgan Stanley">Morgan Stanley</a><sup id="cite_ref-Ind_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ind-61">[61]</a></sup> have been instrumental in pushing up prices in the last five years, with investment in food <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodities</a> rising from $65bn to $126bn (£41bn to £79bn) between 2007 and 2012, contributing to 30-year highs. This has caused price fluctuations which are not strongly related to the actual supply of food, according to the United Nations.<sup id="cite_ref-Ind_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ind-61">[61]</a></sup> Financial institutions now make up 61% of all investment in <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a> futures. According to <a href="/wiki/Olivier_De_Schutter" title="Olivier De Schutter">Olivier De Schutter</a>, the UN special rapporteur on food, there was a rush by institutions to enter the food market following <a href="/wiki/George_W_Bush" class="mw-redirect" title="George W Bush">George W Bush</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Commodities_Futures_Modernization_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Commodities Futures Modernization Act">Commodities Futures Modernization Act</a> of 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-Ind_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ind-61">[61]</a></sup> De Schutter told the Independent in March 2012: "What we are seeing now is that these financial markets have developed massively with the arrival of these new financial investors, who are purely interested in the short-term monetary gain and are not really interested in the physical thing – they never actually buy the ton of wheat or <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>; they only buy a promise to buy or to sell. The result of this financialisation of the commodities market is that the prices of the products respond increasingly to a purely speculative logic. This explains why in very short periods of time we see prices spiking or bubbles exploding, because prices are less and less determined by the real match between supply and demand."<sup id="cite_ref-Ind_61-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ind-61">[61]</a></sup> In 2011, 450 economists from around the world called on the <a href="/wiki/G20" title="G20">G20</a> to regulate the commodities market more.<sup id="cite_ref-Ind_61-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ind-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some experts have said that speculation has merely aggravated other factors, such as <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, competition with <a href="/wiki/Bio-fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Bio-fuels">bio-fuels</a> and overall rising demand.<sup id="cite_ref-Ind_61-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ind-61">[61]</a></sup> However, some such as <a href="/wiki/Jayati_Ghosh" title="Jayati Ghosh">Jayati Ghosh</a>, professor of economics at <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru_University,_Delhi" title="Jawaharlal Nehru University, Delhi">Jawaharlal Nehru University</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Delhi" title="New Delhi">New Delhi</a>, have pointed out that prices have increased irrespective of supply and demand issues: Ghosh points to world wheat prices, which doubled in the period from June to December 2010, despite there being no fall in global supply.<sup id="cite_ref-Ind_61-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ind-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Famine_and_hunger">Famine and hunger</span></h2>
<p>Food deprivation leads to malnutrition and ultimately <a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">starvation</a>. This is often connected with <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>, which involves the absence of food in entire communities. This can have a devastating and widespread effect on human health and mortality. <a href="/wiki/Rationing" title="Rationing">Rationing</a> is sometimes used to distribute food in times of shortage, most notably during times of war.<sup id="cite_ref-Messer_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Messer-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Starvation is a significant international problem. Approximately 815 million people are undernourished, and over 16,000 children die per day from hunger-related causes.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Food deprivation is regarded as a deficit need in <a href="/wiki/Maslow%27s_hierarchy_of_needs" title="Maslow's hierarchy of needs">Maslow's hierarchy of needs</a> and is measured using <a href="/wiki/Famine_scales" title="Famine scales">famine scales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_aid">Food aid</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Food_aid" class="mw-redirect" title="Food aid">Food aid</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Food_aid" class="mw-redirect" title="Food aid">Food aid</a> can benefit people suffering from a shortage of food. It can be used to improve peoples' lives in the short term, so that a society can increase its standard of living to the point that food aid is no longer required.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Conversely, badly managed food aid can create problems by disrupting local markets, depressing crop prices, and discouraging food production. Sometimes a cycle of food aid dependence can develop.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Its provision, or threatened withdrawal, is sometimes used as a political tool to influence the policies of the destination country, a strategy known as <a href="/wiki/Food_politics" title="Food politics">food politics</a>. Sometimes, food aid provisions will require certain types of food be purchased from certain sellers, and food aid can be misused to enhance the markets of donor countries.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> International efforts to distribute food to the neediest countries are often coordinated by the <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Programme" title="World Food Programme">World Food Programme</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Food_safety" title="Food safety">Food safety</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SalmonellaNIAID.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/SalmonellaNIAID.jpg/220px-SalmonellaNIAID.jpg" width="220" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/SalmonellaNIAID.jpg/330px-SalmonellaNIAID.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/SalmonellaNIAID.jpg/440px-SalmonellaNIAID.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1761" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SalmonellaNIAID.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">Salmonella</a> bacteria is a common cause of foodborne illness, particularly in undercooked <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chicken</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chicken_egg" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicken egg">chicken eggs</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HACCP_Seven_Principles.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2a/HACCP_Seven_Principles.png/250px-HACCP_Seven_Principles.png" width="250" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2a/HACCP_Seven_Principles.png/375px-HACCP_Seven_Principles.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2a/HACCP_Seven_Principles.png/500px-HACCP_Seven_Principles.png 2x" data-file-width="578" data-file-height="557" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HACCP_Seven_Principles.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hazard Analysis and Critical Control Points (<a href="/wiki/HACCP" class="mw-redirect" title="HACCP">HACCP</a>) Flowchart</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Foodborne_illness" title="Foodborne illness">Foodborne illness</a>, commonly called "food poisoning", is caused by <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parasite" class="mw-redirect" title="Parasite">parasites</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prion" title="Prion">prions</a>. Roughly 7 million people die of food poisoning each year, with about 10 times as many suffering from a non-fatal version.<sup id="cite_ref-MedlinePlus_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedlinePlus-69">[69]</a></sup> The two most common factors leading to cases of bacterial foodborne illness are cross-contamination of ready-to-eat food from other uncooked foods and improper temperature control. Less commonly, acute adverse reactions can also occur if chemical contamination of food occurs, for example from improper storage, or use of non-food grade soaps and disinfectants. Food can also be adulterated by a very wide range of articles (known as "foreign bodies") during farming, manufacture, cooking, packaging, distribution, or sale. These foreign bodies can include pests or their droppings, hairs, cigarette butts, wood chips, and all manner of other contaminants. It is possible for certain types of food to become contaminated if stored or presented in an unsafe container, such as a ceramic pot with lead-based glaze.<sup id="cite_ref-MedlinePlus_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedlinePlus-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Food_poisoning" class="mw-redirect" title="Food poisoning">Food poisoning</a> has been recognized as a disease since as early as Hippocrates.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The sale of <a href="/wiki/Rancidity" class="mw-redirect" title="Rancidity">rancid</a>, contaminated, or adulterated food was commonplace until the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a>, refrigeration, and vermin controls in the 19th century. Discovery of techniques for killing bacteria using heat, and other <a href="/wiki/Microbiological" class="mw-redirect" title="Microbiological">microbiological</a> studies by scientists such as <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a>, contributed to the modern sanitation standards that are ubiquitous in developed nations today. This was further underpinned by the work of <a href="/wiki/Justus_von_Liebig" title="Justus von Liebig">Justus von Liebig</a>, which led to the development of modern <a href="/wiki/Food_storage" title="Food storage">food storage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Food_preservation" title="Food preservation">food preservation</a> methods.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> In more recent years, a greater understanding of the causes of food-borne illnesses has led to the development of more systematic approaches such as the <a href="/wiki/Hazard_Analysis_and_Critical_Control_Points" class="mw-redirect" title="Hazard Analysis and Critical Control Points">Hazard Analysis and Critical Control Points</a> (<a href="/wiki/HACCP" class="mw-redirect" title="HACCP">HACCP</a>), which can identify and eliminate many risks.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recommended measures for ensuring food safety include maintaining a clean preparation area with foods of different types kept separate, ensuring an adequate cooking temperature, and refrigerating foods promptly after cooking.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Foods that spoil easily, such as <a href="/wiki/Meats" class="mw-redirect" title="Meats">meats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dairy" title="Dairy">dairy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Seafood" title="Seafood">seafood</a>, must be prepared a certain way to avoid contaminating the people for whom they are prepared. As such, the rule of thumb is that cold foods (such as dairy products) should be kept cold and hot foods (such as soup) should be kept hot until storage. Cold meats, such as chicken, that are to be cooked should not be placed at room temperature for thawing, at the risk of dangerous bacterial growth, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">Salmonella</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">E. coli</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allergies">Allergies</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Food_allergy" title="Food allergy">Food allergy</a></div>
<p>Some people have <a href="/wiki/Allergies" class="mw-redirect" title="Allergies">allergies</a> or sensitivities to foods which are not problematic to most people. This occurs when a person's <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a> mistakes a certain food protein for a harmful foreign agent and attacks it. About 2% of adults and 8% of children have a food allergy.<sup id="cite_ref-allergy_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allergy-75">[75]</a></sup> The amount of the food substance required to provoke a reaction in a particularly susceptible individual can be quite small. In some instances, traces of food in the air, too minute to be perceived through smell, have been known to provoke lethal reactions in extremely sensitive individuals. Common food allergens are <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a> (mollusks), <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanuts</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soy" class="mw-redirect" title="Soy">soy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-allergy_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allergy-75">[75]</a></sup> Allergens frequently produce symptoms such as <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rash" title="Rash">rashes</a>, bloating, <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Regurgitation_(digestion)" title="Regurgitation (digestion)">regurgitation</a>. The digestive complaints usually develop within half an hour of ingesting the <a href="/wiki/Allergen" title="Allergen">allergen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-allergy_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allergy-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rarely, food allergies can lead to a <a href="/wiki/Medical_emergency" title="Medical emergency">medical emergency</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Anaphylactic_shock" class="mw-redirect" title="Anaphylactic shock">anaphylactic shock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypotension" title="Hypotension">hypotension</a> (low blood pressure), and loss of consciousness. An allergen associated with this type of reaction is peanut, although <a href="/wiki/Latex" title="Latex">latex</a> products can induce similar reactions.<sup id="cite_ref-allergy_75-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allergy-75">[75]</a></sup> Initial treatment is with <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine" title="Epinephrine">epinephrine</a> (adrenaline), often carried by known patients in the form of an <a href="/wiki/Epi-pen" class="mw-redirect" title="Epi-pen">Epi-pen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Twinject" class="mw-redirect" title="Twinject">Twinject</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_health_issues">Other health issues</span></h3>
<p>Human diet was estimated to cause perhaps around 35% of <a href="/wiki/Diet_and_cancer" title="Diet and cancer">cancers</a> in a human epidemiological analysis by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Doll" title="Richard Doll">Richard Doll</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Peto" title="Richard Peto">Richard Peto</a> in 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> These cancer may be caused by <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogens</a> that are present in food naturally or as contaminants. Food contaminated with fungal growth may contain <a href="/wiki/Mycotoxin" title="Mycotoxin">mycotoxins</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Aflatoxins" class="mw-redirect" title="Aflatoxins">aflatoxins</a> which may be found in contaminated corn and peanuts. Other carcinogens identified in food include <a href="/wiki/Heterocyclic_amine" title="Heterocyclic amine">heterocyclic amines</a> generated in meat when cooked at high temperature, <a href="/wiki/Polyaromatic_hydrocarbon" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyaromatic hydrocarbon">polyaromatic hydrocarbons</a> in charred meat and smoked fish, and <a href="/wiki/Nitrosamine" title="Nitrosamine">nitrosamines</a> generated from nitrites used as food preservatives in <a href="/wiki/Cured_meat" class="mw-redirect" title="Cured meat">cured meat</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Bacon" title="Bacon">bacon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-carcinogens_and_anticarcinogens_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carcinogens_and_anticarcinogens-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Anticarcinogen" title="Anticarcinogen">Anticarcinogens</a> that may help prevent cancer can also be found in many food especially fruit and vegetables. <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">Antioxidants</a> are important groups of compounds that may help remove potentially harmful chemicals. It is however often difficult to identify the specific components in diet that serve to increase or decrease cancer risk since many food, such as beef steak and broccoli, contain low concentrations of both carcinogens and anticarcinogens.<sup id="cite_ref-carcinogens_and_anticarcinogens_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carcinogens_and_anticarcinogens-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet">Diet</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FAO_kcal_his.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/FAO_kcal_his.png/300px-FAO_kcal_his.png" width="300" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/FAO_kcal_his.png/450px-FAO_kcal_his.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/FAO_kcal_his.png/600px-FAO_kcal_his.png 2x" data-file-width="754" data-file-height="565" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FAO_kcal_his.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Changes of food supply (by energy)<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><br />
Other area (Yr 2010)<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> * Africa, sub-Sahara - 2170 kcal/capita/day * N.E. and N. Africa - 3120 kcal/capita/day * South Asia - 2450 kcal/capita/day * East Asia - 3040 kcal/capita/day * Latin America / Caribbean - 2950 kcal/capita/day * Developed countries - 3470 kcal/capita/day</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">Diet (nutrition)</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_and_religious_diets">Cultural and religious diets</span></h3>
<p>Dietary habits are the habitual decisions a person or culture makes when choosing what foods to eat.<sup id="cite_ref-Wansink_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wansink-83">[83]</a></sup> Many cultures hold some food preferences and some food <a href="/wiki/Taboo" title="Taboo">taboos</a>. Dietary choices can also define cultures and play a role in religion. For example, only <a href="/wiki/Kosher" class="mw-redirect" title="Kosher">kosher foods</a> are permitted by <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Halal" title="Halal">halal</a> foods by <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a> is restricted.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> In addition, the dietary choices of different countries or regions have different characteristics. This is highly related to a culture's <a href="/wiki/Cuisine" title="Cuisine">cuisine</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet_deficiencies">Diet deficiencies</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Avitaminosis" class="mw-redirect" title="Avitaminosis">Avitaminosis</a></div>
<p>Dietary habits play a significant role in the health and mortality of all humans. Imbalances between the consumed fuels and expended energy results in either starvation or excessive reserves of <a href="/wiki/Adipose" class="mw-redirect" title="Adipose">adipose</a> tissue, known as body fat.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Poor intake of various vitamins and minerals can lead to diseases that can have far-reaching effects on health. For instance, 30% of the world's population either has, or is at risk for developing, <a href="/wiki/Iodine_deficiency" title="Iodine deficiency">iodine deficiency</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> It is estimated that at least 3 million children are blind due to <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A" title="Vitamin A">vitamin A</a> deficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">Vitamin C</a> deficiency results in <a href="/wiki/Scurvy" title="Scurvy">scurvy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">Calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">Vitamin D</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> are inter-related; the consumption of each may affect the absorption of the others. <a href="/wiki/Kwashiorkor" title="Kwashiorkor">Kwashiorkor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marasmus" title="Marasmus">marasmus</a> are childhood disorders caused by lack of dietary protein.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moral.2C_ethical.2C_and_health-conscious_diets">Moral, ethical, and health-conscious diets</span></h3>
<p>Many individuals limit what foods they eat for reasons of morality, or other habit. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Vegetarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Vegetarian">vegetarians</a> choose to forgo food from animal sources to varying degrees. Others choose a <a href="/wiki/Healthy_diet" title="Healthy diet">healthier diet</a>, avoiding sugars or animal fats and increasing consumption of dietary fiber and <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">antioxidants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">Obesity</a>, a serious problem in the western world, leads to higher chances of developing <a href="/wiki/Heart_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" class="mw-redirect" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, cancer and many other diseases.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> More recently, dietary habits have been influenced by the concerns that some people have about possible impacts on health or the environment from <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">genetically modified food</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Further concerns about the impact of industrial farming (<a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">grains</a>) on <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">animal welfare</a>, human health, and the <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">environment</a> are also having an effect on contemporary human dietary habits. This has led to the emergence of a movement with a preference for <a href="/wiki/Organic_food" title="Organic food">organic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Local_food" title="Local food">local food</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition_and_dietary_problems">Nutrition and dietary problems</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USDA_MyPlate_green.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/USDA_MyPlate_green.svg/220px-USDA_MyPlate_green.svg.png" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/USDA_MyPlate_green.svg/330px-USDA_MyPlate_green.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/USDA_MyPlate_green.svg/440px-USDA_MyPlate_green.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="217" data-file-height="199" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USDA_MyPlate_green.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/MyPlate" title="MyPlate">MyPlate</a> replaced <a href="/wiki/MyPyramid" title="MyPyramid">MyPyramid</a> as the <a href="/wiki/USDA" class="mw-redirect" title="USDA">USDA</a> nutrition guide.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Between the extremes of optimal health and death from <a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">starvation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>, there is an array of disease states that can be caused or alleviated by changes in diet. Deficiencies, excesses, and imbalances in diet can produce negative impacts on health, which may lead to various health problems such as <a href="/wiki/Scurvy" title="Scurvy">scurvy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Osteoporosis" title="Osteoporosis">osteoporosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus_type_2" title="Diabetes mellitus type 2">diabetes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_diseases" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardiovascular diseases">cardiovascular diseases</a> as well as psychological and behavioral problems. The science of nutrition attempts to understand how and why specific dietary aspects influence health.</p>
<p>Nutrients in food are grouped into several categories. Macronutrients are fat, protein, and carbohydrates. Micronutrients are the <a href="/wiki/Mineral_(nutrient)" title="Mineral (nutrient)">minerals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vitamins" class="mw-redirect" title="Vitamins">vitamins</a>. Additionally, food contains water and <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a>.</p>
<p>As previously discussed, the body is designed by <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> to enjoy sweet and fattening foods for evolutionary diets, ideal for <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunters and gatherers</a>. Thus, sweet and fattening foods in nature are typically rare and are very pleasurable to eat. In modern times, with <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">advanced technology</a>, enjoyable foods are easily available to consumers. Unfortunately, this promotes <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a> in adults and children alike.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_definition">Legal definition</span></h2>
<p>Some countries list a legal definition of food, often referring them with the word <i>foodstuff</i>. These countries list food as any item that is to be processed, partially processed, or unprocessed for consumption. The listing of items included as food include any substance intended to be, or reasonably expected to be, ingested by <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>. In addition to these foodstuffs, drink, <a href="/wiki/Chewing_gum" title="Chewing gum">chewing gum</a>, water, or other items processed into said food items are part of the legal definition of food. Items not included in the legal definition of food include <a href="/wiki/Pet_food" title="Pet food">animal feed</a>, live animals (unless being prepared for sale in a market), plants prior to harvesting, medicinal products, <a href="/wiki/Cosmetics" title="Cosmetics">cosmetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a> and tobacco products, <a href="/wiki/Narcotic" title="Narcotic">narcotic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Psychotropic_substance" class="mw-redirect" title="Psychotropic substance">psychotropic substances</a>, and residues and contaminants.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_food">Types of food</span></h2>

<li><a href="/wiki/Organic_food" title="Organic food">Organic food</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Slow_food" class="mw-redirect" title="Slow food">Slow food</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Whole_food" title="Whole food">Whole food</a></li>
</ul>
</div>

Sources referenced in this article say steviosides have up to 250 times the sweetness of sucrose, but others, including stevioside brands such as SweetLeaf, claim 300 times. 1/3 to 1/2 teaspoon (1.6–2.5 ml) of stevioside powder is claimed to have equivalent sweetening power to 1 cup (237 ml) of sugar.</span></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Collingham, E. M. (2011). <i>The Taste of War: World War Two and the Battle for Food</i></li>
<li>Katz, Solomon (2003). <i>The Encyclopedia of Food and Culture</i>, Scribner</li>
<li>Nestle, Marion (2007). <i>Food Politics: How the Food Industry Influences Nutrition and Health</i>, University Presses of California, revised and expanded edition, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0520254031" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 0-520-25403-1</a></li>
<li>Mobbs, Michael (2012). <i>Sustainable Food</i> Sydney: NewSouth Publishing, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781920705541" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 9781920705541</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VQsC7e2qAcY">The Future of Food</a> (2015). A panel discussion at the 2015 <i><a href="/wiki/Digital_Life_Design" title="Digital Life Design">Digital Life Design</a> (DLD)</i> Annual Conference. "How can we grow and enjoy food, closer to home, further into the future? <i><a href="/wiki/MIT_Media_Lab" title="MIT Media Lab">MIT Media Lab</a>’s</i> Kevin Slavin hosts a conversation with food artist, educator, and entrepreneur Emilie Baltz, professor Caleb Harper from <i>MIT Media Lab's</i> CityFarm project, the Barbarian Group's Benjamin Palmer, and Andras Forgacs, the co-founder and CEO of <i>Modern Meadow</i>, who is growing 'victimless' meat in a lab. The discussion addresses issues of sustainable <a href="/wiki/Urban_farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Urban farming">urban farming</a>, ecosystems, technology, food supply chains and their broad environmental and humanitarian implications, and how these changes in food production may change what people may find delicious ... and the other way around." Posted on the official YouTube Channel of <i><a href="/wiki/Digital_Life_Design" title="Digital Life Design">DLD</a></i></li>
</ul>
